Galleries\nSNAPS\nSponsored Photo Galleries\nVideos\nMENU\nBreadcrumb\nHome\nTechnology\nJanuary 04, 2016 11:00 PM\nWi-Fi kiosks coming soon to a former pay phone near you\nMatthew Flamm\nTweet\nShare\nShare\nEmail\nMore\nPrint\nCityBridge\nA Wi-Fi kiosk that offers free Internet service within 150 feet.\nThe citywide network of Wi-Fi stations known as LinkNYC begins its $200 million rollout Tuesday with the unveiling of two "links," or kiosks, on Third Avenue.\nThe 9.5-foot-tall kiosks\xe2\x80\x94one on East 15th Street, the other on East 17th Street\xe2\x80\x94won\'t be functional but they will give the public its first view of the system that will be replacing every pay phone in the city over the next four years.\nPart of Mayor Bill de Blasio\'s plans for narrowing the digital divide, the network will provide free high-speed Internet service for anyone within 150-feet\xe2\x80\x94the minimum range of the signals\xe2\x80\x94of a kiosk.\nAbout 10 links going up Third Avenue will begin providing gigabit-speed Wi-Fi over the next few weeks, according to CityBridge, a consortium that won the city\'s franchise contract in 2014. Advertising on the kiosks will support the gigabit-speed Internet service.\nGigabit speed is 20 to 50 times faster than the broadband found in many homes and 100 times faster than the average public Wi-Fi speed. A gigabit-speed connection would allow a movie to be downloaded in less than a minute, as opposed to more than an hour using a typical public connection.\n"We think it is going to be the fastest Internet experience [many people] have ever had," said Colin O\'Donnell, chief technology officer of CityBridge member-company Intersection, at a press preview with city officials Monday afternoon. "We think there is a huge opportunity to drive change in the industry."\nFormed last year from the merger of outdoor advertising firm (and pay phone operator) Titan and tech firm Control Group, Intersection is leading the rollout as part of a public-private partnership.\nIn February, the trial phase will take another step forward as the kiosks\' tablet component goes live, allowing users to make phone calls\xe2\x80\x94for free within the U.S.\xe2\x80\x94or search the Web. By June more than 500 kiosks, reaching into all five boroughs, will be up and running.\nMore than 4,500 links will be operating within four years.\n"We\'ve been doing a lot of things," said Anne Roest, commissioner of the city Department of Information Technology and Telecommunications, at the press event. "But none of the things we\'ve done have the potential to impact the equity issue of broadband access like Link NYC does."\nRelated Articles\nDe Blasio administration goes after Verizon for failing to deliver FiOS\nVerizon\'s defense doesn\'t ring true\nAmazon to ban sale of Apple, Google video-streaming devices\nInternet providers investigated for slower-than-advertised broadband speeds\nPandit-backed lending startup gets $275 million from Barclays, Macquarie\nAIG invests in worker-protection startup Human Condition Safety\nCuomo presses restart on long-sought Penn Station overhaul\nIBM\'s Watson flexes muscles with Under Armour partnership\nCharter\'s $57 billion Time Warner Cable acquisition approved by state\nIBM leads with most U.S. patents for 23rd year in a row\nView the discussion thread.\nGet our newsletters\nStaying current is easy with Crain\'s New York news delivered straight to your inbox, free of charge\nEmail Address\nCrain\'s Student Offer\nWe offer a discounted rate through Student Beans for students with a valid .edu email address. (CityBridge)\nSay goodbye to New York City\'s payphones as they get replaced with modern-looking Wi-Fi kiosks.\nLinkNYC, a product of CityBridge technology consortium, is rolling out a fiber optic network of kiosks that offer an array of free services including high speed Internet, web browsing, cell phone charging and phone calls to anywhere in the U.S.\nAdvertisement\n"It\'s going to be the largest and fastest public Wi-Fi network in the world," Collin Odonell, managing member of CityBridge, said in a press briefing. "It\'s really the first of this kind of network and it\'s going to transform the streets and people\'s way to access information."\nThe Link kiosks will offer an array of free services including high speed Internet, web browsing, cell phone charging and phone calls to anywhere in the U.S. (CityBridge)\nLink Kiosks will be installed all across the five boroughs including downtown and northern Manhattan, south Bronx, Jamaica, Queens, Flatbush Ave., Brooklyn and St. George, Staten Island. (CityBridge)\nThe $200 million project is expected to bring in $500 million for the city and it won\'t cost taxpayers any money.\nSome of the selected areas to have Links by July include downtown and northern Manhattan, south Bronx, Jamaica, Queens, Flatbush Ave., Brooklyn and St. George, Staten Island.\nThe first two kiosks, which are called "Links" and stand a bit over at 9 feet tall, will be unveiled Tuesday on 3rd Ave. at 15th and 17th street, but they won\'t be fully functional until mid-January.\nEach kiosk will offer up to 1,000 Mbps Internet speeds, which is much higher than the 8.7 Mbps average speed of public Wi-Fi services in the U.S. It\'s also much faster than the average LTE speed, which falls at 6.5 Mbps. The gigabit kiosks will also be able to support hundreds of users using its Wi-Fi up to a 400 foot radius.\nPrivacy might be a concern for many, but the Links will have features that keep users secured.\nUsers will have the option to join a private network that is encrypted and won\'t track them while they browse. Users will also have the option to clear their sessions once they\'re done using the kiosk. Yet, it\'s still recommended not to log into banks accounts over public networks.\nLinkNYC will track usual technical network information such as MAC addresses and other small identifiable information.\nThat might concern some people with being tracked, but CityBridge\'s commitment to net neutrality guarantees that users\' information will remain private and won\'t be shared with any third party.\nThe only exception would be if the information is part of a criminal investigation and it\'s summoned by law enforcement. CityBridge has already put together a Privacy Policy that details what the company does with users\' personally identifiable information.\ntechnology\nAdvertisement\nRecommended', b"You\xe2\x80\x99ll Find The Safest Free Internet Connection With These City Wi-Fi\nFast CompanyCo.DesignTechWork LifeCreativityImpactAudioVideoNewsRecommenderSubscribeHomepageCo.DesignTechWork LifeCreativityImpactAudioVideoNewsRecommenderSubscribeHelp CentercollectionsRecommenderThe apps, books, movies, music, TV shows, and art are inspiring our some of the most creative people in business this monthStrong Female LeadThe struggles and triumphs of prominent women in leadership positionsProductivityProductivity tips and hacksPlatform WarsThe major tech ecosystems that battle for our attention and dollarsTech ForecastWhat\xe2\x80\x99s next for hardware, software, and servicesMost Innovative CompaniesOur annual guide to the businesses that matter the mostMost Creative PeopleLeaders who are shaping the future of business in creative waysWorld Changing IdeasNew workplaces, new food sources, new medicine--even an entirely new economic systemInnovation By DesignCelebrating the best ideas in businessFastCo.Works StudioAn award-winning team of journalists, designers, and videographers who tell brand stories through Fast Company's distinctive lensNewsletterEventsInnovation FestivalThe GrillCurrent IssueCurrent IssueSUBSCRIBEFollow us:advertisementadvertisementadvertisement01.06.16elasticityYou\xe2\x80\x99ll Find The Safest Free Internet Connection With These City Wi-Fi NetworksSan Francisco, San Jose, and now New York City are using much better wireless security than you get at Starbucks or your hotel.\n[Photo: Flickr user Jiuguang Wang] By Sean Captain4 minute ReadThis week, New York City officially inaugurated its free citywide Wi-Fi network, LinkNYC, though it will take several months to get going: 510 access points, called kiosks, are promised\nby July. The city touts some big stats in its fact sheet: 7,550 kiosks to be installed, with 4,550 in the first four years, all providing gigabit connections. Way down at the bottom is the vague phrase, \xe2\x80\x9cLinkNYC offers an encrypted public Wi-Fi network, adding a critical layer of protection to personal data.\xe2\x80\x9d That\xe2\x80\x99s actually the most exciting news in the release: one of the first big uses of a new Wi-Fi technology called Passpoint, aka Hotspot 2.0.advertisementadvertisementPublic Wi-Fi networks are usually about as clean as public toilets. Many offer no security at all. That splash screen you see when you log on at Starbucks asks you to agree to, among other things, the fact that the connection is not secure. \xe2\x80\x9cIn a typical open hotspot\xe2\x80\xa6where you connect and get that web page, that Wi-Fi connection is not encrypted,\xe2\x80\x9d says Kevin Robinson, a VP at the Wi-Fi Alliance, an industry organization that certifies products as supporting the Wi-Fi tech standards. \xe2\x80\x9cThat traffic is happening in the clear.\xe2\x80\x9d In other words, anyone on the network can see what anyone else is doing.What about those coffee shops that write a username and password on the chalkboard next to the seasonal drink specials? \xe2\x80\x9cBelieve it or not, that actually does provide a pretty strong level of security,\xe2\x80\x9d says Robinson. \xe2\x80\x9cEven though they are sharing a username and password, each one of those connections has a unique [encryption] key to the network.\xe2\x80\x9d The question is: Which network? \xe2\x80\x9cThere is an increased\xe2\x80\xa6number of people who would operate fake networks\xe2\x80\xa6 just to collect data,\xe2\x80\x9d says Ond\xc5\x99ej Vl\xc4\x8dek, COO of antivirus maker Avast. They can use the same or a similar name for their bogus network, protected by the same username and password.\nRelated\nThe Top Three Online Security Menaces You Should Worry About In 2016\nLinkNYC and networks already up and running in San Francisco and San Jose provide a new, safer, and much simpler kind of security as part of a tech standard called Passpoint\xe2\x80\x94one of the most important words you probably haven\xe2\x80\x99t heard. Passpoint allows a Wi-Fi hotspot to work roughly the same way that a cellphone tower does. Your connection can switch seamlessly from one hotspot to the next as you move down the street, with no need to log in again to each new station.That\xe2\x80\x99s super-convenient, and can save people a lot of money on their wireless data plans by offloading traffic to Wi-Fi. But Passpoint is a boon even to people who sit next to the same hotspot all day: an individualized secure network connection that (in theory) can\xe2\x80\x99t be spoofed by a hacker. Passpoint actually uses the same security standard, called WPA2, that many access points have offered for years.With Passpoint, rather than signing on to a single access point, you sign on\xe2\x80\x94just once\xe2\x80\x94to the service provider that operates networks of hundreds or thousands of access points; and you take your WPA2 encryption with you. Some networks even have roaming agreements, as cell carriers do. You can automatically and securely hop between San Francisco\xe2\x80\x99s and San Jose\xe2\x80\x99s systems, for example. In addition, a phone, laptop or other device that supports Passpoint holds a list of digital certificates that verify if a network is a legitimate Passpoint provider.Why Isn\xe2\x80\x99t Everyone Using This?The Wi-Fi alliance has been certifying new products that support Passpoint for less than two years. If your laptop, phone, or tablet is older than that, you probably don\xe2\x80\x99t have the capability. (Robinson says that chipmakers may be able to add Passpoint to older gear with a software update.) Even if your device has certified hardware, that doesn\xe2\x80\x99t mean the Passpoint feature has been enabled in the operating system. The rule of thumb is that you need\xe2\x80\x94depending on the device\xe2\x80\x94at least Android 6.0, iOS 7, Mac OS X Mavericks, or Windows 10.advertisementNetworks\xe2\x80\x94especially the free ones\xe2\x80\x94are also slow to upgrade to Passpoint. The ability to hand off from hotspot to hotspot is almost essential for municipal systems like that in San Francisco, as well as for paid global Wi-Fi services like Boingo or Time Warner Cable\xe2\x80\x99s hotspot network (which both recently upgraded to Passpoint). But it doesn\xe2\x80\x99t offer much to Joe\xe2\x80\x99s coffee shop or Jane\xe2\x80\x99s hotel, where users are stationary. Yes, the Passpoint security is much better, but it\xe2\x80\x99s not as if most people will skip their soy latte because the caf\xc3\xa9 doesn\xe2\x80\x99t offer a new Wi-Fi standard they probably haven\xe2\x80\x99t even heard of. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m not aware of any, what you may call a sole proprietor [such as a] caf\xc3\xa9 deploying a Passpoint network,\xe2\x80\x9d says Robinson.Instead, the next place you will see Passpoint is probably via cellphone providers, such as T-Mobile, which are already conducting trials of the technology. \xe2\x80\x9cAs those trials conclude, we\xe2\x80\x99ll expect to see additional Passpoint deployments,\xe2\x80\x9d says Robinson.So what\xe2\x80\x99s the takeaway for itinerant web users? Best-case scenario: Connect to the nearest Passpoint network if your town has one and your device supports it. Even if you are sitting right next to the router in your caf\xc3\xa9, go for the public hotspot across the street, if it\xe2\x80\x99s a street in San Francisco or San Jose or (soon) New York. Somewhat more likely scenario: Connect to a network that requires a username and password. Otherwise, you have a box of lesser options. The best is to subscribe to a virtual private network (VPN) service such as Cloak or HMA (or the VPN your company may supply) that creates an encrypted connection for you\xe2\x80\x94protecting your data even if it\xe2\x80\x99s running through a hacker\xe2\x80\x99s fake Wi-Fi hotspot. At the very least, make sure the website you are going to uses the HTTPS address that signals web encryption. \xe2\x80\x9cUltimately, people are going to make their own decisions on how much risk they want to assume,\xe2\x80\x9d says Robinson.advertisementadvertisementadvertisementAbout the authorSean Captain is a Bay Area technology, science, and policy\njournalist. Follow him on Twitter\n@seancaptain. But my iPhone is on fire. After connecting to one of LinkNYC\'s gigabit wireless hotspots, the futuristic payphone replacements that went live for beta testing this morning, I\'m seeing download speeds of 280 Mbps and upload speeds of 317 Mbps (based on Speedtest\'s benchmark). To put it in perspective, that\'s around ten times the speed of the average American home internet connection (which now sits at 31 Mbps). And to top it all off, LinkNYC doesn\'t cost you a thing.\nGallery: Hands-on with LinkNYC | 9 Photos\n9\n+5\nOf course, my experience was a charmed one. The LinkNYC hotspot (or Link, for short) I tested went live just a few hours before I arrived, and there were only a handful of other people on it. I was also standing right in front of it -- speeds will vary depending on your distance, as well as the type of device you\'re using. Still, seeing Speedtest\'s bandwidth meter rocket to 300 Mbps was astonishing. It felt like I was staring at the future of urban internet access in my cold, gloved hand.LinkNYC is the culmination of New York City\'s big push to modernize payphones. Its tall and thin kiosks feature gigabit fiber connections (yes, most are plugged right into fiber lines) delivered via 802.11ac WiFi, USB charging, free voice calls, and a tablet for internet access, maps and directions. They also sport two large screens which display ads (the only way LinkNYC is making money, at the moment), as well as public service announcements. Wireless range clocks in at 150 feet, though as always, that may be affected by obstacles, windows and walls. Each kiosk also offers a private, fully encrypted network accessible by devices that support Hotspot 2.0 (at this point, LinkNYC only supports Apple gear).Signing onto a Link for the first time was surprisingly easy, as far as WiFi onboarding goes. After joining the "LinkNYC Free Wi-Fi" network, you just need to plug in your email address into a landing page to get connected. If you\'ve got a Hotspot 2.0 device, you\'ll automatically get a prompt to install a security key for the the private LinkNYC network. I had no trouble connecting to the kiosk with my MacBook Air while sitting in the Starbucks right in front of it.The process was a bit more complex for my iPhone, since I wanted to test out LinkNYC\'s encrypted offering. After receiving the prompt to install the additional security key, I had to manually approve it and restart my WiFi before my phone phone would hop onto the private network. Unfortunately, while I wanted to test out LinkNYC\'s estimated range this morning, my iPhone ended up dying before I could (likely because I had it exposed to the extreme cold for a while).On my MacBook Air, I saw speeds of around 70 Mbps up and down while sitting just a few feet from the Link. That speed was likely being limited by my laptop\'s connection to AOL\'s VPN, which can\'t be disabled easily. Being indoors definitely slowed things down a bit as well, but that\'s still insane speed for free WiFi. It\'s likely a great deal faster than your home internet connection, unless you\'re paying for an expensive 100 Mbps plan.Even with slower speeds at greater distances, I wouldn\'t be surprised if some New Yorkers end up dumping their home ISP and using Links full-time instead. LinkNYC representatives say they won\'t stop anyone from doing that, though they might have to throttle data hogs who end up ruining the experience for others. Links could also end up being a major gateway to the internet for millions of New Yorkers who can\'t afford service at home.When it comes to security, LinkNYC won\'t be able to detect anything you\'re doing on its private network. On its public network, though, it\'ll be able to track your MAC address. (Update: Representatives tell me LinkNYC won\'t track any web traffic on its public or private network.) The full privacy policy, published by NYC\'s Department of Information Technology and Telecommunications (DOITT), notes that Citybridge (the consortium behind LinkNYC) might share anonymized data with third-parties, but not anything personally identifiable.While there are only a handful of kiosks installed at the moment, LinkNYC plans to have 500 up and running by July. They\'ll remain in beta testing until around 200 units are deployed, according to representatives, and by 2020 the group plans to have at least 4,450 kiosks in the wild. It\'s tough to tell if LinkNYC will reach its long-term goals, but the fact that it was able to develop and deploy its kiosks in about a year is impressive.\nIn this article:\nculture,\ngear,\ngigabitWiFi,\ninternet,\nLinkNYC,\nNYC,\nWiFi\nAll products recommended by Engadget are selected by our editorial team, independent of our parent company. He spent several years fixing other people\'s computers before he started down the treacherous path of writing about technology. ClubDeadspinGizmodoJalopnikJezebelKotakuLifehackerSplinterThe TakeoutThe RootThe OnionClickholeThe InventorynullNYC's New Public Wifi Is Obscenely FastBryan Lufkin1/19/16 3:20pmFiled to: citiesFiled to: citiescitieslinknycnew yorkwifiinternetinfrastructure10415EditEmbiggenSend to EditorsPromoteShare to KinjaToggle Conversation toolsChange in-article videoGo to permalinkToday, the first of New York City\xe2\x80\x99s public, gigabit wifi hotspots opened to the public. I tried them, so take it from me: They\xe2\x80\x99re insanely fast. How fast? Fast enough that Starbucks\xe2\x80\x99 free internet is about to get killed. Earlier this month, we reported on LinkNYC, the Big Apple\xe2\x80\x99s initiative to roll out breakneck internet access to everyone. Today, the first four modules are open in beta mode to the public.Advertisement The 9.5-foot-tall rectangles, which are replacing the city\xe2\x80\x99s pay phones, feature USB outlets for charging your devices (actual charger not included), city maps, 911 emergency access, and video calls to anywhere in the country. And, of course, wi-fi. Fast wi-fi. I ran internet speed tests at various locations this morning, with the goal of comparing a NYCLink location (at 15th Street and 3rd Avenue) to other common spots where I often score free wi-fi. Those included a Starbucks, a New York City subway platform that offers gratis wireless, and the Gawker Media offices. The winner? LinkNYC. It wasn\xe2\x80\x99t even close. Like, at all.AdvertisementHere\xe2\x80\x99re the results of a test I ran on the LinkNYC public wifi:AdvertisementAnd at the Starbucks located right next to the Link:AdvertisementFinally, from the F and M train platform at 14th Street:AdvertisementLinkNYC is ten times faster than New York\xe2\x80\x99s existing public internet, and infinitely quicker than Starbucks. With city internet that fast, what\xe2\x80\x99s going to happen to Starbucks, Barnes & Noble, Panera, or other establishments whose complementary wifi is such a pull? After all, if you want to work or study at a place that isn\xe2\x80\x99t the library or your house, you pretty much have to buy a mocha and camp out. And with the sheer number of Links that New York is planning to install\xe2\x80\x947,500 units in the next 12 years, each with a service radius of up to 400 feet\xe2\x80\x94it could mean that you and your devices will never be outside a serviceable zone.AdvertisementStill. These things aren\xe2\x80\x99t designed to have people convene in front of them to stream Orange Is the New Black en masse. (Although the folks at LinkNYC have said the system can handle that.) They\xe2\x80\x99re designed for temporary, fleeting experiences: checking your email, downloading a map of the city, making a quick Skype call. AdvertisementWhether LinkNYC will complicate the city\xe2\x80\x99s relationship with chains and businesses remains to be seen. For now, the units are still being tested (911, calls, and maps aren\xe2\x80\x99t yet available), and they seem to be advertisers more than anything, with signs hawking Coors Light and Citibank. You can use the wi-fi, of course\xe2\x80\x94you just need to input an email address. (LinkNYC says that it will never give out private user information.) And in case you\xe2\x80\x99re wondering, no sites are blocked from the network. Want to download some sleaze on a park bench? Go for it...I guess?AdvertisementFor now, the city wants to open up ten new Links a day, with the goal of 500 running by July. Let the mass, ultra-fast streaming begin. The first LinkNYC hub was installed in December, but today is the first day New Yorkers have been able to use it to connect to the internet.\nToday\'s rollout is also a test of the LinkNYC network, which provides newly laid fiber to each hub. The network is still officially in beta, and the group anticipates some changes as the network expands, but the current version is significantly faster than many commercially available connections. A Verge test found both upload and download speeds faster than 300 Mbps. "The device is usually the bottleneck," said Miles Green, head of network engineering at Intersection.\nConnecting to the network takes users to a login screen, shown below, where they are asked to provide their email. LinkNYC officials say emails are collected for informational purposes only and will not be sold or shared, in accordance with the system\'s privacy policy. Notably, that single login will keep users connected across the entire system, regardless of which hub is closest at a given time.\nThe current rollout is proceeding up Third Avenue, but organizers say LinkNYC booths will begin the trek up Eighth Avenue over the next several weeks. By mid-July, the city plans to have 500 such hubs installed throughout the city. When the project is complete, it will include more than 7,500 hubs throughout the five boroughs. Each hub will include two USB charging ports, touchscreen web browsing, and two 55-inch ad displays.\nMore From\nThe Verge\nGood Deals\nThe 12.9-inch iPad Pro is cheaper than ever today\nPlus, deals on the 11-inch model, the S10 lineup of phones, and more\nXbox One controllers, natively supported in iOS 13, are much cheaper today\nPlus, save on Logitech PC accessories and Samsung\xe2\x80\x99s Galaxy S10 lineup\nMore in Good Deals\nVerge3.0_Logomark_Color_1\nSign up for the\nnewsletter\nCommand Line\nCommand Line delivers daily updates from the near-future.\nEmail (required)\nSubscribe\nBy signing up, you agree to our Privacy Policy and European users agree to the data transfer policy.\nThis Article has a component height of 8. MagazineSectionsFashionArt & DesignTravelFoodCultureSearchSearch\xe2\x86\x92\nShare Text 43\nTech\nPersonal Technology: Review\nThe Future of Public Wi-Fi: What to Do Before Using Free, Fast Hot Spots\nLike New York, cities all over are getting speedy public Wi-Fi, but proceed with caution\nLike New York City's phone-booths-turned-hotspots, public Wi-Fi is getting faster and more widespread. The WSJ's Joanna Stern tells you what you need to know before logging on. Photo/video: Drew Evans/The Wall Street Journal.\nBy\nJoanna Stern\nJoanna Stern\nThe Wall Street Journal\nBiographyJoanna Stern\n@JoannaStern\nJoannaStern\njoanna.stern@wsj.com\nJan. 19, 2016 2:43 pm ET\nI\xe2\x80\x99m considering moving my office to the bus stop on 17th and 3rd in Manhattan. Sure, it\xe2\x80\x99s brutally cold right now and the bathroom across the street isn\xe2\x80\x99t the cleanest but, oh, the Wi-Fi. It isn\xe2\x80\x99t only free, it\xe2\x80\x99s faster than anything else around, including my real office\xe2\x80\x99s network. The best thing about the neighborhood? It\xe2\x80\x99s one of the first parts of New York City to turn old phone booths into hot spots. In the months ahead, they\xe2\x80\x99ll cover the city like graffiti in the 80s, meaning I can walk around town using fast, free Wi-Fi\xe2\x80\x94not...\nTo Read the Full Story\nSubscribe\nSign Galleries\nVideos\nMENU\nBreadcrumb\nHome\nTechnology\nMarch 02, 2016 11:00 PM\nPay phone conversions bridge digital divide and won't cost taxpayers a dime\nMaya Wiley and Jen Hensley\nTweet\nShare\nShare\nEmail\nMore\nPrint\nTell us if this sounds familiar: You wake up, reach for your smartphone and begin scrolling through email, headlines and social media. Within minutes you know what is happening in your office, your neighborhood and the world at large. Throughout the day, you continue to interact with a series of devices that keep you socially and professionally connected.\nFor a lot of us, this describes our routine. But for too many New Yorkers, it doesn\xe2\x80\x99t even come close.\nIn 2011, the United Nations declared access to broadband Internet a basic human right and a global priority. Five years later, 34 million Americans still lack access to broadband. The digital divide affects even the most tech-savvy cities, including New York. One in four New Yorkers lacks access to high-speed Internet at home. That\xe2\x80\x99s more than 2.5 million people who can\xe2\x80\x99t search for a job, do homework or manage their health care without going to a library or a McDonald's.\nMobile access is often seen as a cheaper and more convenient substitution to household Internet. For those who can afford it, unlimited data means ubiquitous connectivity. For those who can\xe2\x80\x99t, data is a prized commodity. Shelling out an extra $60 a month on top of cellular charges simply isn\xe2\x80\x99t an option for the many New Yorkers who already pinch pennies just to afford rent, food and child care. And overage charges of $15 per gigabyte can throw a tight household budget into crisis.\nAs\xc2\xa0Mayor Bill de Blasio rolls out universal computer-science classes in public schools, it is our obligation to ensure that our kids can get online to become 21st-century innovators, no matter their zip code. And the mayor\xe2\x80\x99s investment in free, high-speed broadband to more than 21,000 public housing residents will bring broadband access to some of the New Yorkers who need it most.\nThe latest effort, LinkNYC, can make a huge difference for families and individuals who can\xe2\x80\x99t afford more data\xe2\x80\x94but still need the Internet for nearly every aspect of their lives.\nBy replacing New York City\xe2\x80\x99s pay phones with more than 7,500 super-fast, free Wi-Fi hotspots, or \xe2\x80\x9cLinks,\xe2\x80\x9d we will dramatically increase access to free broadband Internet across the five boroughs. These Links will also provide other free services, including domestic phone calls, a tablet for Internet browsing, 911 and 311 calls, and USB device charging. Last month, we launched LinkNYC\xe2\x80\x99s beta phase in Manhattan, and by summer there will be more than 500 Links installed across all five boroughs.\nThe LinkNYC network has the potential to help millions of New Yorkers get and stay connected even if they lack a mobile device or just can\xe2\x80\x99t afford expensive data plans.\nLinkNYC represents a new kind of public-private partnership, one that leverages the innovation and agility of the private sector without costing taxpayers or users a dime. In fact, with its robust network of over 12,000 digital advertising displays, LinkNYC will generate more than half a billion dollars in revenue for New York City over the next 12 years.\nIt\xe2\x80\x99s a model other cities can and should emulate. By delivering a free, premium user experience, we will create more economic opportunity, capacity for innovation, educational access and civic participation. And yes, there will still be enough bandwidth to download that HD cat video, too.\nMaya Wiley is counsel to the mayor. Jen Hensley is general manager of LinkNYC.\nRelated Articles\nQuestion for pols: Who's going to pay for all this?\nRikers reimagined: Innovative ideas to turn the infamous island into a New York\xe2\x80\xa6\nU.S. cannot make Apple unlock an iPhone, at least in Brooklyn drug case\nSolution to the city's old call-box problem is right in front of us\nView the discussion thread.\nGet our newsletters\nStaying current is easy with Crain's New York news delivered straight to your inbox, free of charge\nEmail Address\nCrain's Student Offer\nWe offer a discounted rate through Student Beans for students with a valid .edu email address. Galleries\nSNAPS\nSponsored Photo Galleries\nVideos\nMENU\nBreadcrumb\nHome\nTechnology\nMarch 17, 2016 12:00 AM\nThe Wi-Fi kiosks replacing New York pay phones will soon pop up in another city\nMatthew Flamm\nTweet\nShare\nShare\nEmail\nMore\nPrint\nPeter D\'Amato\nA LinkNYC kiosk on Third Avenue in Manhattan.\nSidewalk Labs is taking its LinkNYC show on the road.\nIn a conference call Monday, CEO Dan Doctoroff announced that the Alphabet-owned company will be installing more than 100 Wi-Fi-enabled kiosks in the city that wins the Smart City Challenge, an initiative of the U.S. Department of Transportation. A winner will be announced in June.\nThe program, for medium-size cities with populations of up to 850,000, will award up to $40 million for building a smart transportation system that ultimately includes connected vehicles and self-driving cars.\nThe kiosks, modeled after the LinkNYC network that is currently replacing New York City\'s old pay-phone infrastructure, will take up approximately 25 city blocks. They will be part of a transportation data and analytics platform called Flow, Doctoroff said on the call along with Department of Transportation Secretary Anthony Foxx.\nSidewalk Labs will be developing Flow in the coming months with the Smart City program\'s seven finalists, which Foxx announced at the South by Southwest festival last week. They include Denver, Austin, San Francisco and Portland, Ore.\n"Flow offers unprecedented citywide transportation analytics to help cities understand congestion and identify areas underserved by transit using aggregated anonymized data," Foxx said on the call.\nFlow will collect data from mobility apps and from Wi-Fi kiosks and help city governments and residents better manage traffic and test new options like ride-sharing and other transportation alternatives.\nSidewalk Labs, which is building LinkNYC through its Intersection subsidiary, has said that it wants to expand its Wi-Fi hotspot technology beyond New York. And Flow, which will be provided gratis to the winning metropolis, will become another one of Sidewalk Labs\' offerings as it begins working with other cities.\nCiting the pressure that poor transportation options put on low-income communities, Doctoroff said that technology offers cities a rare chance to make current infrastructure more workable.\n"We know we\'re not going to build more roads," Doctoroff said. "It\'s hard to invest in meaningfully more mass transit. We have to do better with what we have."\nRelated Articles\nConsumers coming over to Apple\'s side in dispute with feds\nWeWork to raise $430 million for expansion in Asia\nTop local venture capitalist sides with Obama on Apple encryption fight\nGoogle veteran joins Oscar as chief technology officer\nPATH joins MTA and Amtrak in banning hoverboards\nCUNY, Cornell Tech launch women-in-tech initiative\nDaily fantasy sites agree to stop taking bets in New York\nIPhone hearing canceled as FBI tests hack without Apple help\nView the discussion thread.\nGet our newsletters\nStaying current is easy with Crain\'s New York news delivered straight to your inbox, free of charge\nEmail Address\nCrain\'s Student Offer\nWe offer a discounted rate through Student Beans for students with a valid .edu email address. Skepticism and Relief - The New York Times\nSectionsSEARCHSkip to contentSkip to site indexNew YorkSubscribeLog InLog InToday\xe2\x80\x99s PaperNew York|New Yorkers Greet the Arrival of Wi-Fi Kiosks With Panic, Skepticism and ReliefAdvertisementSupported byNew Yorkers Greet the Arrival of Wi-Fi Kiosks With Panic, Skepticism and ReliefImageEdgardo Arroyo, 47, used the LinkNYC kiosk at 142nd Street and Broadway this month. Mr. Arroyo said he lives across the street and had run out of available minutes on his phone plan.CreditCreditBenjamin Norman for The New York TimesBy Patrick McGeehanJuly 26, 2016When it comes to acceptance of New York City\xe2\x80\x99s rapidly growing network of sidewalk kiosks offering \xe2\x80\x9cfree super fast Wi-Fi,\xe2\x80\x9d some people are Nekeya Browns and some are Alex Padillas.As soon as the LinkNYC booths were activated in their Washington Heights neighborhood this month, Ms. Brown celebrated by plugging in her headphones and swaying to some Marvin Gaye tunes; Mr. Padilla, in his Yankees jersey, stood a few feet back, reluctant even to touch the keyboard for fear of having his pocket of personal data picked.Mr. Padilla, 60, said his primary concern was \xe2\x80\x9chaving to give them information,\xe2\x80\x9d not that he was certain who \xe2\x80\x9cthey\xe2\x80\x9d were. Ms. Brown, a young woman in brightly patterned tights, was practically giddy as she appraised a glowing kiosk on West 142nd Street in Manhattan.\xe2\x80\x9cWhoever thought of this was a great person,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, listing all of the benefits of the kiosks. \xe2\x80\x9cI told a homeless lady that whenever you need to call your family, you can use this.\xe2\x80\x9dAnd so it goes in the first stage of the citywide rollout of these curbside machines that promise swift connections to the internet, phone service and ports for charging cellphones and other devices, all at no cost to the users. With the activation this week of the first units to be installed in Queens, there will be about 300 online, almost all of them in Manhattan.Along Eighth Avenue in Midtown, some homeless people are camping around the kiosks. Sascha Freudenheim, who runs a consulting firm on West 36th Street, said the way some people were using the kiosks \xe2\x80\x9cseems completely counter to their purpose.\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Freudenheim said he thought they would be used to help people find their way around the city, not serve as gathering spots.Residents of more residential neighborhoods have complained that the kiosks are too bright, too loud and more attractive to idle squatters than they are to busy passers-by in need of a quick connection. Other New Yorkers are demonstrating their usual skepticism, eyeing the kiosks warily and wondering what the catch is.Aniya Lee, 16, frequently uses the kiosk at 183rd Street and Creston Avenue in the Bronx, but only to play songs on YouTube on the video screen. She said she did not feel comfortable using the Wi-Fi or connecting her phone to the USB outlet.\xe2\x80\x9cFor some reason, I don\xe2\x80\x99t trust it,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cThey\xe2\x80\x99re trying to get everyone\xe2\x80\x99s information.\xe2\x80\x9dCity officials are hoping that the public\xe2\x80\x99s long-term relationship with the tall, slim kiosks settles in somewhere between standoffish and clingy. They admit they did not know what to expect; no other city has tried to create anything quite as extensive as LinkNYC.\xe2\x80\x9cThis is an unprecedented project,\xe2\x80\x9d said Anne Roest, commissioner of the city\xe2\x80\x99s Department of Information Technology and Telecommunications. \xe2\x80\x9cWe are going to have to make adjustments to make them better. A lot of these things are things that really we weren\xe2\x80\x99t anticipating when we went live.\xe2\x80\x9dIf all goes according to the plan, the kiosks will be as commonplace as pay phones once were: more than 7,500, spread throughout every neighborhood of the city. Most of them will replace former pay phones, though the company installing them has begun choosing additional sidewalk sites.That company, actually a consortium known as CityBridge, is behind schedule on the rollout. Under its franchise agreement with the city, more than 500 kiosks were to be working by this month, in all five boroughs.ImageAlex Padilla, 60, looked over the LinkNYC kiosk at 135th Street and Broadway. Mr. Padilla said he had no use for the kiosks.CreditBenjamin Norman for The New York TimesCityBridge executives said their progress was slowed by several factors, including the strike by Verizon employees and a lawsuit filed by Telebeam, a company that controlled about 1,300 pay phones in the city. Telebeam challenged the city\xe2\x80\x99s granting of an exclusive franchise to CityBridge; a federal judge dismissed the suit this month.Jen Hensley, the general manager of LinkNYC, said some former pay phone sites had proven unsuitable for the kiosks. In those instances CityBridge has started seeking approvals from local community boards to place the machines in other spots.So far, most of the kiosks have been placed along Third and Eighth Avenues in Manhattan, aligned with fiber-optic cable already laid to provide fast wireless connections to the internet. After Queens, the network will extend to Brooklyn and then to Staten Island, Ms. Hensley said.CityBridge has only just begun to assess how successful the system is, Ms. Hensley said. But city officials said they were pleased with the early use and the adjustments CityBridge had made, including lowering the maximum volume at night in response to noise complaints.Nearly 275,000 people have registered to use the kiosks, which requires users to provide an email address. Once a smartphone is registered, it will automatically connect to the Wi-Fi signals that radiate from the kiosks and extend 100 feet or more.Close to one million of those connections are being made weekly, according to CityBridge statistics. In July, users initiated about 40,000 sessions on the built-in computer screens and made about 23,000 phone calls per week.Last weekend, experts on digital data and online privacy were wondering what CityBridge planned to do with all of the personal information made available. Benjamin Dean, a fellow in cybersecurity and internet governance at Columbia University, and Mariko Hirose, a senior staff attorney for the New York Civil Liberties Union, raised that question at a gathering of hackers in Manhattan.The civil liberties organization has asked city officials to revise LinkNYC\xe2\x80\x99s privacy policy, arguing that it allows the system\xe2\x80\x99s operators to collect all sorts of data about users and sell it to other companies. Ms. Hensley said CityBridge executives were working with city officials on revising the current policy.\xe2\x80\x9cWe don\xe2\x80\x99t have plans to collect any additional data in the future,\xe2\x80\x9d Ms. Hensley said.Contrary to some people\xe2\x80\x99s fears, she said, the consortium is barred from sending ads to users\xe2\x80\x99 phones.\xe2\x80\x9cWe know way less about you than your mobile phone provider does,\xe2\x80\x9d she said.CityBridge maintains that its business model involves selling ads on the large, lighted panels on the sides of the kiosks, essentially small billboards that will line the busiest blocks of the city. It has pledged to share at least $500 million in ad revenue with the city over the first 12 years.But Mr. Dean said he was doubtful, in part because of the involvement of Google\xe2\x80\x99s parent company, Alphabet. It owns Sidewalk Labs, an investor in one of the members of the CityBridge consortium.\xe2\x80\x9cWhat Google\xe2\x80\x99s doing here is taking the business model that they\xe2\x80\x99ve perfected in the online world and bringing it into the real world,\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Dean said in an interview.Despite the warnings of critics like Mr. Dean, city officials seem determined to push ahead with a network that they say will provide valuable benefits, to people much like Jose Soto, a 35-year-old Washington Heights resident.On a hot day in early July, Mr. Soto squatted in the shade near a LinkNYC kiosk, typing texts on his basic cellphone to his wife in Panama. \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t pay my internet this month,\xe2\x80\x9d he said with a shrug. \xe2\x80\x9cThis is good for a lot of people.\xe2\x80\x9dSamantha Schmidt contributed reporting. LinkNYC\nTo Court a Skeptical Public, New York Sends Wi-Fi Ambassadors\nEillie Anzilotti\nAug 12, 2016\nAs LinkNYC rolls out through the city, guides take to the streets to demonstrate how to use the free network. Share Tweet LinkedInEmailPrint\nMANHATTAN, NEW YORK \xe2\x80\x94 On a scorching morning, Stefan Carpio, a 26-year-old Bronx native, stands on the northwest corner of 150th and Broadway in Washington Heights, wearing a black T-shirt bearing this invitation: \xe2\x80\x9cAsk me about Link.\xe2\x80\x9d\nOK, what\xe2\x80\x99s Link?\nCarpio gestures to a sleek sidewalk kiosk equipped with a small touchscreen. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s got everything you would expect from your mobile phone or tablet right here\xe2\x80\x94maps, the internet, calling capability,\xe2\x80\x9d Carpio says. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd of course, there\xe2\x80\x99s the free high-speed Wi-Fi.\xe2\x80\x9d\nThe kiosk is one node in the network that makes up LinkNYC, New York City\xe2\x80\x99s initiative to bring free internet to the city streets. New York is the first city to attempt this model of public web access, and since the program launched in January, residents are still acclimating to the presence of the monolith-like Link outposts. \xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99s a lot of confusion,\xe2\x80\x9d Carpio says. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve seen people just stop and stare at the machines.\xe2\x80\x9d\nAs a member of the LinkNYC Street Team, which has been showing passersby the ropes of the network since the first machines were installed, Carpio brings a human face to New York\xe2\x80\x99s far-flung tech overhaul. A stack of instructional flyers under his arm, he mans the sidewalk in front of the kiosk, called out to the scattered morning crowds and offering them a quick tutorial on how to use the console. Carpio demonstrates how to browse the internet, charge a mobile phone, place a free call, or navigate through the city with the help of Google Maps. The interface is Android-esque; the wireless network extends out from the booth in a 150-foot radius, and passersby can log into the free network on their phones.\nTo date, around 350 Link kiosks have emerged from the graves of old pay phones; more than 350,000 people have registered to the network. The deployment cost over $200 million, but the network will come at no additional cost to the city or taxpayers; it\xe2\x80\x99s funded by ads that rotate across the screens on the sides of the kiosk. Over the course of an eight-year rollout, the number of kiosks will grow to anywhere from 7,500 to 10,000 throughout the five boroughs, pulling the number of network registrations up exponentially and generating over $500 million in advertising revenue, according to Jen Hensley, the general manager for LinkNYC.\nFor many, the presence of a Link kiosk is a mild convenience: one less trip into a Starbucks to mooch off the free Wi-Fi, or a chance to place a call from something larger than a deck of cards. There\xe2\x80\x99s a headphone jack, so you can dial the number on screen and place the call directly through the kiosk.\n"Forgot my phone this morning so I\'m calling my mom for her birthday. She thinks it\'s cool." - Chris, Hells Kitchen pic.twitter.com/5vC0Z1lwEw\n\xe2\x80\x94 LinkNYC (@LinkNYC) August 3, 2016\nBut for a substantial number of New Yorkers, public-facing technology is not just a plaything, but a bridge. Carpio joined the LinkNYC street team after completing a course in software testing at Per Scholas, a Bronx-based national nonprofit that connects unemployed or low-income people with free tech training skills. Prior to the course, Carpio says, he struggled to find a job; as one of the dozen or so Per Scholas graduates that now make up the LinkNYC street team, Carpio now works two five-hour shifts per week at the most recently installed kiosks, patiently walking people through the new additions to the neighborhood.\nPer Scholas managing director Kelly Richardson says the organization enrolls students of color at a rate of 90 percent. One-third are women; all come from low-income families. The student body in the Bronx center, Richardson says, \xe2\x80\x9creally looks like New York.\xe2\x80\x9d\nThe internet access gap in NYC is greatest in black and Hispanic homes. And it\xe2\x80\x99s getting wider. \xc2\xa0But it\xe2\x80\x99s the part of New York that\xe2\x80\x99s been left out of the increasingly internet-connected fabric of the city. According to a 2015 report from the Office of the New York City Comptroller, 26 percent of households in the city lack broadband internet access at home. That access gap is greatest in black and Hispanic homes, and demarcated along socioeconomic lines. And it\xe2\x80\x99s getting wider: The report notes that \xe2\x80\x9cthe digital divide appears to be growing on a year-over-year basis, with poor neighborhoods of the South Bronx and Central and Eastern Brooklyn witnessing a decrease in the percentage of households with broadband.\xe2\x80\x9d\nThe idea behind LinkNYC is to bridge these access gaps. Street team members like Carpio are working to ensure people are aware of the kiosks\xe2\x80\x94and how to use them safely.\nCities are changing fast. Keep up with the CityLab Daily newsletter. The best way to follow issues you care about.\nSubscribe\nLoading...\nFrom the time of its initial announcement, LinkNYC has been met with skepticism about the omnipotence of its public-private ownership. The program is a joint venture between the City of New York and an amalgamation of tech companies called CityBridge, which, if one peels back the Russian-nesting-doll layers of ownership far enough, traces back to Google.\nThe fact that the Silicon Valley behemoth would be on the receiving end the location-tracking and web-browsing data from the Link network has been cause for concern\xe2\x80\x94particularly because, as The Atlantic previously reported, \xe2\x80\x9cpeople who can\xe2\x80\x99t afford broadband access would be most likely to take advantage of a fast, free option for internet access like LinkNYC. And\xe2\x80\xa6low-income communities are already among the most surveilled in the country.\xe2\x80\x9d\nIn March, the New York Civil Liberties Union addressed a letter to the office of Mayor Bill de Blasio, calling for heightened security across the network. In response, Link asserted the data collected will not be sold or distributed; any data shared with advertisers and the city would be made anonymous.\nCurrently, LinkNYC offers two connectivity options on mobile phones: the regular open network, and an encrypted, private network. The latter is more secure, but for the time being, available only on the newest Apple devices\xe2\x80\x94a disparity that drives another wedge between the socioeconomic sectors that Link aims to bridge.\nHowever, Carpio says he mostly sees people use the kiosks to make calls or look up directions, though he once helped someone search for a job. According to Link, the average network login session lasts around three minutes, not long enough to leave a substantial data trail. And street team members like Carpio are on hand to explain safe browsing practices (such as sticking to URLs that begin with \xe2\x80\x9chttps\xe2\x80\x9d) to boost privacy.\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis a completely new use of the technology\xe2\x80\x94it doesn\xe2\x80\x99t exist anywhere else,\xe2\x80\x9d says LinkNYC GM Hensley. \xe2\x80\x9cWe knew that people would be curious about it, and that it might not be the most intuitive thing.\xe2\x80\x9d But as communication channels increasingly shift online, it\xe2\x80\x99ll only be more imperative that it\xe2\x80\x99s equally accessible to all New Yorkers. Someday, these Wi-Fi kiosks could be as familiar a part of the city\xe2\x80\x99s streetscape as phone booths were to New Yorkers of the 1970s.\nAbout the Author\nEillie Anzilotti\n@eillieanzi\nFeed Eillie Anzilotti is a former editorial fellow at CityLab.\nExpand Comments +\nMost Popular\nEnvironment Paris Wants to Grow \xe2\x80\x98Urban Forests\xe2\x80\x99 at Famous Landmarks The city plans to fill some small but treasured sites with trees\xe2\x80\x94a climate strategy that may also change the way Paris frames its architectural heritage.\nFeargus O\'Sullivan\nJun 19, 2019\nTransportation The World\'s 15 Most Complex Subway Maps As ranked by a research team of mathematicians and theoretical physicists.\nEric Jaffe\nFeb 25, 2016\nDesign How Birds and Bees Survive in the City Pollinators\xe2\x80\x94the wildlife that shuffle pollen between flowers\xe2\x80\x94are being decimated. But they may still thrive with enough help from urban humans.\nAriel Aberg-Riger\nJun 21, 2019\nTransportation America Would Happily Pay Uber An Extra $7 Billion Economists put a (big) number on the ride service\xe2\x80\x99s consumer surplus in 2015.\nLaura Bliss\nSep 15, 2016\nDesign Can This Flawed Brutalist Plaza in Boston Be Fixed? The chain-link fences are finally down at Boston\xe2\x80\x99s long-closed Government Services Center, thanks to some clever design updates.\nMark Byrnes\nJun 21, 2019\nMapsClick Here\nNewslettersClick Here\nFacebookClick Here\n\xc3\x97\nSpecial\xc2\xa0Reports FAQ Masthead Solutions Store Atlantic\xc2\xa0Media Jobs Media\xc2\xa0Kit Partner Solutions Contact\xc2\xa0Us Privacy Terms\xc2\xa0&\xc2\xa0Conditions\nSign up for CityLab Daily:\nSign Up\nLoading...\nSubscribe to our other newsletters here.\nCityLab is committed to telling the story of the world\xe2\x80\x99s cities: how they work, the challenges they face, and the solutions they need.Citylab.com \xc2\xa9 2019 The Atlantic Monthly Group', b"LinkNYC: Free Wi-Fi use at kiosks surpasses 576,000 people | am New York\nMenu\nLATEST PAPER\nSEARCH\nNews\nPolitics\nThings To Do\nEAT & DRINK\nEntertainment\nReal Estate\nTransit\nOpinion\nLATEST PAPER\n79\xc2\xb0\n6/23/2019\nGood Afternoon\nSEARCH\nHome\nNews\nPolitics\nElections\nThings To Do\nEat & Drink\nEntertainment\nCelebrities\nPets\nSecrets of NY\nReal Estate\nCity Living\nBrooklyn\nManhattan\nQueens\nStaten Island\nTransit\nSports\nOpinion\nJim Marguiles\nCartoon roundup\nWeather\n79\xc2\xb0\n6/23/2019\nGood Afternoon\nNews\nLinkNYC: Free Wi-Fi use at kiosks surpasses 576,000 people\nA\nLinkNYC\nkiosk, at 3rd Avenue and 15th Street in Manhattan, on Wednesday, Feb. 17, 2016.\nPhoto Credit: Linda Rosier\nBy Ivan Pereira ivan.pereira@amny.com\n@IvanPer4\nUpdated October 4, 2016 12:00 AM\nPrint\nShare\nfbShare\nTweet\nEmail\nIt\xe2\x80\x99s pretty fly for free Wi-Fi.\nThe administrators of LinkNYC revealed that 576,321 people have signed into the internet service with their devices at the 400 Wi-Fi kiosks that have already replaced the old pay phones in three boroughs. The number of subscribers has surpassed the entire populations of Miami, Atlanta or Oakland.\nJen Hensley, the general manager of LinkNYC, said those 500,000+ users have used the service more than 26 million times since it launched in February, and expects that number to grow as they install 7,500 kiosks in the five boroughs over the next few years.\nrecommended reading\nSay goodbye to web browsing at LinkNYC kiosks\nThe modern-day \xe2\x80\x9cphone booths\xe2\x80\x9d stirred controversy over its free web access on its tablets which were hogged by users at certain locations. Last month, administrators disabled tablet web access and said reports of monopolizing kiosks dropped 82 percent.\nThey added that tablet use has gone up 12 percent\xc2\xa0since the change as more users take advantage of the tablet\xe2\x80\x99s other applications, such as maps, 311 and of course, phone calls.\nStarting Wednesday, the LinkNYC tablet will also have an app for New Yorkers to register to vote. The app will link directly to the state\xe2\x80\x99s DMV Electronic Voter Registration Application website until the Oct. 14 deadline.\nGet Breaking News emails\nThe latest about your city, as it happens.\nSign up\nBy clicking Sign up, you agree to our privacy policy.\nBy Ivan Pereira ivan.pereira@amny.com\n@IvanPer4\nIvan has been a staff reporter with amNewYork since May 2012 and covers breaking news, politics and enterprise stories.\nShare on Facebook\nShare on Twitter\nComments\nWe're revamping our Comments section. All rights reserved.", b"NYC Wi-Fi kiosks try to turn video watchers into voters | am New York\nMenu\nLATEST PAPER\nSEARCH\nNews\nPolitics\nThings To Do\nEAT & DRINK\nEntertainment\nReal Estate\nTransit\nOpinion\nLATEST PAPER\n79\xc2\xb0\n6/23/2019\nGood Afternoon\nSEARCH\nHome\nNews\nPolitics\nElections\nThings To Do\nEat & Drink\nEntertainment\nCelebrities\nPets\nSecrets of NY\nReal Estate\nCity Living\nBrooklyn\nManhattan\nQueens\nStaten Island\nTransit\nSports\nOpinion\nJim Marguiles\nCartoon roundup\nWeather\n79\xc2\xb0\n6/23/2019\nGood Afternoon\nOpinionColumnistsMark Chiusano\nBy Mark Chiusano\nNYC Wi-Fi kiosks try to turn video watchers into voters\nLinkNYC\nkiosks are experimenting.\nPhoto Credit: Linda Rosier\nUpdated October 4, 2016 6:48 PM\nPrint\nShare\nfbShare\nTweet\nEmail\nNew York City\xe2\x80\x99s much-touted LinkNYC kiosks were meant to replace public telephones with free Wi-Fi, tablet browsing and calls. But predictably, New Yorkers mostly liked standing in front of them to watch music videos.\nSome, reportedly, managed to find a little porn, though as one woman charging her phone at an Eighth Avenue kiosk said Tuesday, the browser wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t even let you get to Rotten.com, let alone porn being easily accessible. Rotten.com, a shock site, \xe2\x80\x9clet\xe2\x80\x99s you see crazy things,\xe2\x80\x9d she explained, acknowledging the street wasn\xe2\x80\x99t really the place for it. She declined to give her name, along with her two companions also charging their phones and sitting on a flipped-over Village Voice distribution container.\nThe porn/music-video/people-gathering nexus, combined with the particular people who often gathered to use the tablets \xe2\x80\x94 mostly homeless people and some mentally ill \xe2\x80\x94 resulted in complaints. Then last month, LinkNYC shut down all tablet-based web-browsing. The consortium that manages the kiosks released some preliminary findings since that experiment this week.\nTo browse, or not to browse is the question\nIts numbers suggest that more people could be getting their turn to call, check out maps, look at 311, and the other similarly banal tasks that are left when the internet is shut off. In the 11 days before web-browsing was turned off vs. the 11 days after, browsing sessions increased 12 percent.\nSome kiosk users queried on Tuesday didn\xe2\x80\x99t find the lack of browsing too problematic. Gill Diaz, 50, a part-time forklift operator from the Bronx, says he\xe2\x80\x99s tried to make important phone calls from time to time and people \xe2\x80\x9care out here watching videos.\xe2\x80\x9d\nBut others predictably bemoaned the loss, like the three women sharing a Village Voice container and charging station on Eighth Avenue. One, in a poster example of the purposes of accessible browsing, said she looked up sites and info about potential jobs before the switch.\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t understand,\xe2\x80\x9d she repeated multiple times when considering the change. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat did they expect?\xe2\x80\x9d\nSign up for amExpress, the conversation starter for New Yorkers.\nSign up\nBy clicking Sign up, you agree to our privacy policy.\nIt\xe2\x80\x99s a reasonable question. Expanding internet access was one of the (laudable) goals of the kiosk program. While that didn\xe2\x80\x99t necessarily mean physical outposts, the tablets ended up bringing the internet to people who have trouble getting it elsewhere \xe2\x80\x94 who are kicked out of Starbucks or McDonald\xe2\x80\x99s for trying to charge their phones, and briefly had surprisingly unfettered access in the one place that was their domain: the street.\nThat experiment made the streets too chaotic, in the view of some, so the entities that truly own the street \xe2\x80\x94 the city and its vocal businesses and citizens, not its homeless and others who spend lots of time on the sidewalk \xe2\x80\x94 shut it off.\nTurning watchers into voters\nBefore they did, it was a brief demonstration of internet habits in its own chaos put out in the open.\nPerhaps hoping to add a new draw to the kiosks now that the internet is gone, LinkNYC is launching a homepage allowing users to register to vote. Will civic duty be as big a draw as music videos or free porn?\nActually, it\xe2\x80\x99s not such a far-fetched connection. The ever-entrepreneurial website Pornhub recently announced that during the Sept. 26 presidential debate traffic to its website \xe2\x80\x9cfell as much as 16% compared to an average Monday in this time period.\xe2\x80\x9d The site often tracks porn-viewing fluctuations during live events and sometimes finds similar dips \xe2\x80\x94 during the Euro 2016 soccer final in July and the NBA Finals Game 7 in June, for example.\nThose NYC free internet users might not be that different from America\xe2\x80\x99s voting populace. But the voters presumably did their browsing at home.\nBy Mark Chiusano\nMark Chiusano is a member of the Newsday and amNew York editorial board.\nShare on Facebook\nShare on Twitter\nComments\nWe're revamping our Comments section. (TIMOTHY A. CLARY/AFP/Getty Images)\nApparently porn surfing isn\'t the only activity the city\'s free Web kiosks are good for.\nA month after the kiosks\' web browsing capabilities were disabled \xe2\x80\x94 following rampant complaints about people hogging them to oogle porn \xe2\x80\x94 officials say they\'ve seen a 12% rise in usage.\nAdvertisement\nThat increase is boosted by more New Yorkers using the kiosks\' tablets to make phone calls, access 311, and get web-based directions from navigation services, according to LinkNYC, the private company the city contracted with to launch the services.\nLast week alone the kiosks were used to make 21,000 free phone calls, according to Link NYC.\nAt the same time, calls to the company complaining that people were hogging the kiosks dropped 82%, LinkNYC said.\nThe city announced it was disabling the web browsing capabilities last month because of complaints of people monopolizing the machines \xe2\x80\x94 including well-publicized incidents of people watching porn.\nThe hundreds of kiosks, which are located in Manhattan, the Bronx and Queens, are replacing outdated payphones and includes tablets and Wi-Fi hot spots free of charge.\nOfficials said Tuesday that as of the end of September, 576,321 people had used the Wi-Fi services \xe2\x80\x94 more than the population of Atlanta, Miami, and Oakland.\nlinknyc\ntechnology\nAdvertisement\nRecommended', b"The 27 Most Innovative Spaces And Places Of The Year\nFast CompanyCo.DesignTechWork us:advertisementadvertisementadvertisement10.11.16innovation by designThe 27 Most Innovative Spaces And Places Of The YearFrom hospitals and houses to subway signs and parks, these projects are reshaping the world around us.\nBy Katharine Schwab9 minute ReadHow can cities build infrastructure today to support tomorrow\xe2\x80\x99s rapidly increasing urban population, which the UN says will only increase in the coming decades? Over the past year, we saw designers, architects, and urban planners hard at work finding new ways to think about housing and improve public spaces. They range from new construction\xe2\x80\x93micro homes and prefab houses\xe2\x80\x93to various forms of adaptive reuse, with derelict urban spaces finding second lives as environmentally friendly parks and arts centers.advertisementadvertisementThe winners and finalists in the Spaces, Places, and Cities category of Co.Design\xe2\x80\x98s Innovation by Design Awards exemplify the move toward focused, thoughtful design for communities. Here are the 27 best projects of the year.Winners\nCarmel Place\nFirm: nARCHITECTSCarmel Place is New York City\xe2\x80\x99s first micro-unit building, where modular units averaging 300 square feet were prefabricated off-site and then stacked to create a tower of compact, tiny living spaces. So life doesn\xe2\x80\x99t feel quite so cramped for those inside, the building devotes more space to communal amenities, like a gym and public roof terrace. It\xe2\x80\x99s one alternative to the housing shortages gripping many expensive cities, New York included.\nHunts Point Landing\nFirm: Mathews Nielsen Landscape ArchitectsMeet the micro-park. This 100-foot-wide park and pier in the South Bronx brought new life to a tiny sliver of land located in an industrial zone and polluted shoreline as part of the South Bronx Greenway Master Plan. Mathew Neilsen Landscape Architects transformed a dead-end street into 1.5 acres of vibrant, community-minded green space, taking on the New York City bureaucracy to make it happen.Finalists\n\xe2\x80\x9cLaying The Groundwork\xe2\x80\x9d Design Guidelines\nFirm: Design Trust For Public SpaceadvertisementPublished by the Design Trust for Public Space in partnership with the N.Y.C. Department of Housing Preservation and Development, this design bible acts as a guide to the intricacies of urban design. Its nine core commandments focus on the ground floor of buildings in particular, exploring how retail and community spaces can be integral parts of every community. The guide gives architects and developers a set of best practices on how details like bench placement and electrical service can make them more usable and even more social.\n1.8 London\nFirm: Studio EchelmanThis web-like sculpture suspended over London\xe2\x80\x99s Oxford Circus is actually a visualization of the 2011 T\xc5\x8dhoku earthquake and tsunami in Japan. At night, visitors can use their smartphones to influence the patterns of light projected onto the dynamic sculpture, which is named for the number of microseconds the day was shortened when the earthquake hit.\nBiodesign Studio\nFirm: Local ProjectsThis permanent exhibition at the Tech Museum of Innovation in San Jose, California, allows visitors to gain a deeper understanding of synthetic biology, bioengineering, and biological design. How? Hands-on, interactive labs let visitors do things like hack live bacteria\xe2\x80\x99s DNA to make it change color or \xe2\x80\x9ccreate\xe2\x80\x9d their own digital creatures. It takes an incredibly complex emerging field of science, and makes it tangible through space and exhibition design.\nBlackbirds\nFirm: Bestor ArchitectureadvertisementThis cluster of 18 homes in the Los Angeles hills could be an answer to the future of urban housing. The homes were designed to maximize the number of single-family homes in a given area. The two- or three-bedroom homes fit together to look like a single house, complete with a courtyard and shared outdoor spaces, increasing density without appearing to do so.\nBrooklyn Boulders Urban Design And Architecture Strategy\nFirm: Brooklyn BouldersRock climbing gyms are on the up-and-up\xe2\x80\x93but could they also serve as community hubs? One New York-based mainstay, Brooklyn Boulders, builds its gyms in neglected places, providing colorful, art-covered climbing walls in old factories and unused churches. But these are more than exercise spaces\xe2\x80\x93the company also uses its facilities for community events and concerts.\nChicago Riverwalk\nFirm: Ross Barney Architects, Sasaki\nThis open pedestrian walkway along the Chicago lakefront has been in the works since 2001, and the latest major addition adds three distinct sections of public space. There\xe2\x80\x99s the Marina, where pedestrians can watch boats come and go while they dine or stroll. The Cove, with boating and kayak rentals. And the River Theater\xe2\x80\x93a public amphitheater right on the water. It\xe2\x80\x99s a car-free pedestrian environment, designed to handle the Windy City\xe2\x80\x99s annual flooding while still providing a beautiful place to walk along the water.\nClover\nFirm: Mathieu Lehanneur\nadvertisementThis design for Paris\xe2\x80\x99s streetlights is a refreshing update to the city\xe2\x80\x99s classic art nouveau posts, with digitally manufactured wooden bases made of different grains that represent cities across the country, and efficient LEDs that are partially powered by a solar dome on top of the entire structure. Tr\xc3\xa8s chic!\nHavemeyer Building\nFirm: Common LivingCommon is in the middle of the coliving craze, providing housing for young urban professionals who want maximum flexibility and minimum hassle. You can apply online for one of the Havemeyer Building\xe2\x80\x99s 51 private bedrooms (stocked with Casper mattresses), and management keeps all the shared spaces clean and stocked with necessities. Say good-bye to buying toilet paper or fighting over who takes out the trash.\nConnected Worlds\nFirm: Design I/OThis immersive installation inside the New York Hall of Science\xe2\x80\x99s Great Hall Building is like a giant, virtual world (complete with a 45-foot waterfall) built out of projectors and Kinect cameras that visitors can manipulate. Connected Worlds is designed to help kids understand the trade-offs and advantages of sustainability as they explore a wildly complex, abstracted environment much like our own.\n\xe2\x80\x9cForest Of Light\xe2\x80\x9d\nFirm: Sou FujimotoadvertisementCreated for the fashion label COS, this installation from architect Sou Fujimoto uses a bare minimum of materials to create an immersive spatial experience. Architectural beams of light paired with sound, fog, and mirrored walls converge as a mystical environment that reacts to visitors.\nForest Of Light, The Asia Culture Center\nFirm: Kyu Sung Woo ArchitectsThis park in the city of Gwangju, South Korea, has exhibition spaces, libraries, performance halls, and a children\xe2\x80\x99s museum\xe2\x80\x93not to mention gardens, playgrounds, a tree park, and a grand lawn. It\xe2\x80\x99s all in service of revitalizing the city\xe2\x80\x99s identity and providing it with a cultural centerpiece.\nBut the complex also serves as a memorial and monument to the Gwangju Uprising, when hundreds of people died revolting against the dictator Chun Doo-hwan in 1980.\nGenerator Amsterdam\nFirm: DesignAgencyDo all hostels have to be dingy? Not according to Generator Amsterdam. Located in the Dutch capital\xe2\x80\x99s hip Oost neighborhood, this hostel is full of lounges, bars, and designer wallpaper\xe2\x80\x93with no lumpy mattresses or bedbugs in sight.\nHudson Yards Public Square And Gardens\nFirm: Nelson Byrd Woltz Landscape ArchitectsadvertisementThis elevated public park, which will be situated over New York City\xe2\x80\x99s new Hudson Yards neighborhood, is being built on a steel platform on top of old railroad tracks. In short, it\xe2\x80\x99s a park that floats. And to support the growth of 30,000 plants and 200 mature trees, it will use jet engines for ventilation, a 60,000-gallon irrigation system, and \xe2\x80\x9csmart\xe2\x80\x9d soil that will allow trees to grow in just four feet of dirt.\nLinkNYC\nFirm: IntersectionNew York City is in the process of replacing its 7,500 antiquated booths with new kiosks that offer free Wi-Fi, phone calls, and device charging, all powered by digital advertisements on the sides of the kiosks. Even though they\xe2\x80\x99ve been controversial since their rollout, LinkNYC offers a vision for the future of connectivity in cities.\nMaggie\xe2\x80\x99s Centre Barts\nFirm: Steven Holl ArchitectsHospitals rarely feel like healing spaces. The U.K.-based Maggie\xe2\x80\x99s Centres provide comfortable environments for cancer patients to heal, without the clinical coldness for which hospitals are so often known. The latest location in London takes that dedication to warmth and recovery to the next level, with a public roof garden that features space for yoga and tai chi and a beautiful translucent facade inspired by musical notes. It\xe2\x80\x99s a new vision for how hospital architecture should look and function.\nMaven\nFirm: GMadvertisementCar-sharing services have their perks, but they can\xe2\x80\x99t ensure that your next car will already be tuned to your favorite radio station or the chair set to the position that most suits you. This experimental program from GM allows car-sharers to set up their preferred settings via an app, which can communicate with the OnStar system inside the next car they\xe2\x80\x99ll be driving. The app also allows the user to control the interior settings on their smartphone while inside the car, regardless of what make or model they\xe2\x80\x99re sitting in.\nMercedes-Benz Stadium\nFirm: HOKThe new home stadium for the Atlanta Falcons has a semi-transparent retractable roof that resembles a camera\xe2\x80\x99s aperture, a striking departure from most stadiums\xe2\x80\x99 roofs, which mimic the design of a convertible car. When closed, the roof hosts a large screen to envelop fans in 360-degree video. The finished stadium will receive LEED certification\xe2\x80\x93a first for professional sports arenas.\nMuji Hut\nFirm: MujiThe Japanese lifestyle company\xe2\x80\x99s first prefabricated house follows its broader design ethos. It\xe2\x80\x99s highly functional, with just enough design to be beautiful as well. The three houses are simply adorned but aesthetically pleasing, their compact design revealing how the company envisions its products, both small and large, augmenting urban lifestyles.\nRockaway Beach Boardwalk\nFirm: Pentagram, WXYadvertisementAfter Hurricane Sandy took out a chunk of Rockaway Beach\xe2\x80\x99s boardwalk in Queens, Pentagram stepped in to help rebuild the walkway. The agency was in charge of signage, which it built right into the new boardwalk, with massive 150-by-50 foot letters that can even be read by planes in the air. To beachgoers, the words \xe2\x80\x9cRockaway,\xe2\x80\x9d composed of a subtle blue hue, blend into the walkway, a striking combination of signage and environmental design.\nSquare, Inc. Headquarters\nFirm: Bohlin Cywinski JacksonThe mobile payment company\xe2\x80\x99s San Francisco headquarters are housed in what was once a windowless data center for supercomputers\xe2\x80\x93but you\xe2\x80\x99d never know it. The company\xe2\x80\x99s four floors are grounded by a central staircase that takes the city boulevard as its inspiration, with Square-enabled vendors selling coffee and sandwiches along the edges of these \xe2\x80\x9cstreets.\xe2\x80\x9d\nStarter Home*\nFirm: Office Of Jonathan TateCities and suburbs are littered with wasted spaces that are too small for conventional homes. That\xe2\x80\x99s where Starter Home* comes in, with a strategy to transform these vacant lots into micro homes that a wider range of people can afford. A 195-square-foot proof-of-concept settles between a duplex and an industrial warehouse in New Orleans, and there are more Starter Homes on the way in New Orleans, Austin, Houston, Pittsburgh, and Oakland.\nThe Kathleen Grimm School For Leadership And Sustainability At Sandy Ground\nFirm: Skidmore, Owings & MerrilladvertisementNew York\xe2\x80\x99s first net-zero-energy school uses 50% less energy than your average New York City public school to serve nearly 450 elementary schoolers. How? It\xe2\x80\x99s all about efficiency and lighting. The building uses photovoltaic solar panels to collect energy, and is oriented along a strategic \xe2\x80\x9cL\xe2\x80\x9d shaped plan to increase daylighting. Plenty of skylights and reflective ceiling panels take advantage of every last drop of natural light, too.\nThe Smithsonian National Museum Of African American History & Culture\nFirm: Adjaye AssociatesBritish starchitect David Adjaye designed Washington\xe2\x80\x99s latest addition to the National Mall\xe2\x80\x93a museum dedicated to the history and culture of African-Americans in the United States. The multilayered facade resembles an inverted ziggurat, covered in detailed ironwork made by African-American artists that\xe2\x80\x99s designed to allow light into the museum\xe2\x80\x99s interiors.\nThe Strand, American Conservatory Theater (A.C.T.)\nFirm: Skidmore, Owings & MerrillThe Strand is a former 725-seat abandoned movie theater that was given new life as the home for the American Conservatory Theater in San Francisco. It\xe2\x80\x99s a perfect example of preserving a building\xe2\x80\x99s history and character while repurposing it to a new purpose: The renovations preserved the building\xe2\x80\x99s facade and some of the original decorations, marquee letters, and even graffiti, but the interior is decidedly modern, with a giant LED screen dominating the lobby.\n[Photo: Craig Dugan Photography]\nUniversity Of Minnesota Health, Clinics & Surgery Center\nFirm: CannonDesignadvertisementThis University of Minnesota\xe2\x80\x99s health center reimagines what a hospital can be, with no private offices or front desk. Instead, patients are checked in via iPad by staff members who come to them\xe2\x80\x93and rooms are available by reservation. It\xe2\x80\x99s a new way of thinking about how space can be effectively used to promote healing. No one likes going to the hospital, but details like these can go a long way to improving the experience.advertisementadvertisementadvertisementAbout the authorKatharine Schwab is an associate editor based in New York who covers technology, design, and culture. are\ngrowing in\npopularity, according to the city.\nPhoto Credit: AFP / Getty Images / Timothy A. Clary\nBy Ivan Pereira ivan.pereira@amny.com\n@IvanPer4\nUpdated January 18, 2017 12:00 AM\nPrint\nShare\nfbShare\nTweet\nEmail\nThe city is set to announce Wednesday that more than 1.04 million users have logged into LinkNYC kiosks.\nThe Wi-Fi hot spots started to roll out a year ago and caught on quickly despite complaints over users who were monopolizing the free internet and tablet features, according to Jen Hensley, LinkNYC\xe2\x80\x99s general manager.\nOver 555 terabytes have been downloaded through LinkNYC since it launched, according to statistics compiled by the city. The terminal in front of Macy\xe2\x80\x99s in Herald Square had the most use in the five boroughs with over 600,000 sessions as of Jan. 4, the city said.\nrecommended reading\nOfficial: Porn viewers are hogging free web kiosks\nRoughly 537 kiosks have been installed so far, with at least one kiosk in each borough and more are on the way this year. The city plans to install 7,500 kiosks in total.\nThe service ran into some controversy over the summer following complaints of users hogging the tablets for hours and using its built in Web browser for questionable content. In response, the city removed the web access function from the terminals in September.\nOther stats from the city included:\n\xe2\x80\x94New Wi-Fi sign-ups per week: 40,000\nGet Breaking News emails\nThe latest about your city, as it happens.\nSign up\nBy clicking Sign up, you agree to our privacy policy.\n\xe2\x80\x94Wi-Fi sessions per week: 4 million\n\xe2\x80\x94Busiest day by users: Jan 4, 2017, with 125,756 unique users\n\xe2\x80\x94Busiest day by data transferred: Dec 31, 2016, with 4.7 terabytes\nBy Ivan Pereira ivan.pereira@amny.com\n@IvanPer4\nIvan has been a staff reporter with amNewYork since May 2012 and covers breaking news, politics and enterprise stories.\nShare on Facebook\nShare on Twitter\nComments\nWe're revamping our Comments section.
